<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_24dffd3b-7007-4222-9f16-b6820d9e7bdd'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer13211179106864416755">My book club read this book last month. This is how book club went basically: <br/><br/>Joy: Colleen, what was your favorite part of this book?<br/><br/>Me: Well, Joy, I'll get to that in a few, but would anyone like some chocolate? (Passes around a bowl of chocolate until they are all staring at me expectantly. Reluctantly continues.) You know, Joy, Stephen King never disappoints. Every time I turned the page, there were more words that formed sentences. The kind of sentences that make up all of Stephen King's boo</span>
  <span id="freeText13211179106864416755" style="display:none">My book club read this book last month. This is how book club went basically: <br /><br />Joy: Colleen, what was your favorite part of this book?<br /><br />Me: Well, Joy, I'll get to that in a few, but would anyone like some chocolate? (Passes around a bowl of chocolate until they are all staring at me expectantly. Reluctantly continues.) You know, Joy, Stephen King never disappoints. Every time I turned the page, there were more words that formed sentences. The kind of sentences that make up all of Stephen King's books. Long ones, short ones, incomplete ones. But that's the beauty of this book, right? The sentences tell a story in a way that only sentences could. And THAT is why this book was so brilliant. <br /><br />Joy: You're a f*cking idiot. Why are you even in book club if you never actually read the books? <br /><br />Me: Five stars! <br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_1543342284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1543342284" />
    <a title="megs_bookrack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48328025-megs-bookrack"><img alt="megs_bookrack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532027727p2/48328025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1543342284?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="megs_bookrack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="megs_bookrack" href="/user/show/48328025-megs-bookrack">megs_bookrack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781529311136" href="/book/show/51184185-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48328025-megs-bookrack?shelf=stephen-king-read">stephen-king-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1543342284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9104061717742309476"><u>Readers be warned:</u> <i>Rose Madder</i> opens with one of the <b>most vivid scenes of violence</b> I have ever read; harsh, brutal and heartbreaking in its ferocity. <br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1598470378ra/30020006.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This novel tells the compelling tale of an average, suburban housewife, Rosie Daniels, as she makes the decision to, and subsequently, leaves her vicious husband, Norman.<br/><br/>Admittedly, I started this book on two prior occasions and just couldn't make it past that first scene. <br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1591105757ra/29582904.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I literally found it to be mentally disturbing, therefore, I would put it do</span>
  <span id="freeText9104061717742309476" style="display:none"><u>Readers be warned:</u> <i>Rose Madder</i> opens with one of the <b>most vivid scenes of violence</b> I have ever read; harsh, brutal and heartbreaking in its ferocity. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1598470378ra/30020006.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This novel tells the compelling tale of an average, suburban housewife, Rosie Daniels, as she makes the decision to, and subsequently, leaves her vicious husband, Norman.<br><br>Admittedly, I started this book on two prior occasions and just couldn't make it past that first scene. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1591105757ra/29582904.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I literally found it to be mentally disturbing, therefore, I would put it down and then just avoid it.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1585932730ra/29225522.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This doesn't mean I thought it was bad. I didn't. I think it is a testament to King's writing, how much those few pages effected me.<br><br>I knew if I kept going, I would enjoy the book, I just needed to be in the right head space in order to do so.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1514134184ra/24753146.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Even for King, those first pages are <b>brutal and shattering</b> because you know this type of thing happens in real life, to real people. <br><br>This isn't a book where the villain is an alien, an ancient spirit, or a ghost; he is just a man. A corrupt cop with a penchant for violence against women.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1585932730ra/29225519.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>One of my favorite aspects of King's writing is his ability to fully develop his characters. <br><br>That is certainly the case here. From Rosie and Norman, all the way to the smallest side characters, I felt like I knew them all.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1491335610ra/22400121.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Rosie married Norman when she was very young, just out of high school. She had lost her family and had no outside support system. <br><br>He became her whole world, no matter how difficult that world was.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1585932730ra/29225521.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>After she decides to leave, she discovers just how sheltered she has become. Her ability to navigate the outside world is close to nonexistent. <br><br>I felt bad for her. I wished I was there to help her and guide her.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1466700639ra/19510059.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>She hasn't a clue where she will go or what she'll do. She has no skills. <br><br>What will she do for a job? How will she survive without money?<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1531760116ra/25982697.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In spite of her reservations, however, Rosie knows if she doesn't leave, Norman will kill her. It's not an if, it's a when.<br><br>Fleeing for her life, she boards a bus and selects the farthest stop she can afford. Once there, she will try to build a new life for herself with no resources. She left everything behind.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1580936168ra/28909872.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Unfortunately, if there is one thing Norman is good at, besides hitting, it's tracking people down. He's humiliated by Rosie walking out on him and vows to find her. <br><br>Now his sole mission, he has big plans to make her pay. No matter what it takes, no matter how many laws he has to break, he will not give up.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1485604778ra/21831493.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The intensity that builds over the course of this book is truly something special. The narrative alternates back and forth between Rosie and Norman, so you can track his progress, and that really helped to build the sense of dread.<br><br>I became quite attached to Rosie rather quickly, as did many she met in her new life. That definitely escalated my fear for what I suspected was coming.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1585932730ra/29225520.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In addition, there was a really interesting fantastical element thrown in that reminded me a bit of a cross between <i>The Picture of Dorian Gray</i> and <i>The Dark Tower</i> series. <br><br>That may seem like a really odd combination, and it is, but in King's skilled hands, it somehow works.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1513088743ra/24661481.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I truly enjoyed this story. Thank you to all my Constant Reader friends who pushed me to finally pick it up this year. <br><br>I found it to be <b>empowering and moving</b>. I loved Rosie so much and her growth was satisfying. I am very happy I made time for it and can absolutely see myself reading it again some day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9104061717742309476" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5056480491336518259"><b>3.5 Stars</b><p> If you can handle a story of <b>horrific</b> abuse, Hannibal Lector's idea of a <b>tasty</b> dinner, and <b>haunted</b> dreams from the beyond, you're going to love <b>ROSE MADDER.</b></p><p> Norman "Bates" Daniels is a <b>lunatic</b> cop with a foul mouth and a sick, evil mind, and for wife Rose, a life of <b>fear</b> and <b>pain</b> begins the night of her honeymoon.......for the reader, it's almost immediate and a real shocker.</p><p> Unprepared, terrified and alone, after 14 long years of living in hell, Rose finally flees for her life knowing</p></span>
  <span id="freeText5056480491336518259" style="display:none"><b>3.5 Stars</b><p> If you can handle a story of <b>horrific</b> abuse, Hannibal Lector's idea of a <b>tasty</b> dinner, and <b>haunted</b> dreams from the beyond, you're going to love <b>ROSE MADDER.</b></p><p> Norman "Bates" Daniels is a <b>lunatic</b> cop with a foul mouth and a sick, evil mind, and for wife Rose, a life of <b>fear</b> and <b>pain</b> begins the night of her honeymoon.......for the reader, it's almost immediate and a real shocker.</p><p> Unprepared, terrified and alone, after 14 long years of living in hell, Rose finally flees for her life knowing full well <b>he will never stop searching.</b></p><p><b> ROSE MADDER</b> is an intense thriller throughout most of the 600+ pages, but for me lost its momentum when we entered the mysterious, but drawn out world of the supernatural, and the ending.....just ok.</p><p> As always, enjoyed the tie-ins to other <b>KING</b> novels.</p></span>

<div id="review_2517761586" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2517761586" />
    <a title="Johann (jobis89)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50893023-johann-jobis89"><img alt="Johann (jobis89)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588540681p2/50893023.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2517761586?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johann (jobis89)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johann (jobis89)" href="/user/show/50893023-johann-jobis89">Johann (jobis89)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670858699" href="/book/show/335370.Rose_Madder">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2517761586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17476254469948861802">"It ain't the blows we're dealt that matter, but the ones we survive."<br/><br/>A single drop of blood causes Rose McClendon to come to the realisation that her husband might actually kill her. So she ups and leaves him, setting off to a new city...<br/><br/>I always get really excited for the King novels where the main protagonist is a female as I've a pretty good track record with them - Lisey's Story, Dolores Claiborne, Gerald's Game etc. I'm pretty sure these were all 5 star reads for me! So I had high expectat</span>
  <span id="freeText17476254469948861802" style="display:none">"It ain't the blows we're dealt that matter, but the ones we survive."<br /><br />A single drop of blood causes Rose McClendon to come to the realisation that her husband might actually kill her. So she ups and leaves him, setting off to a new city...<br /><br />I always get really excited for the King novels where the main protagonist is a female as I've a pretty good track record with them - Lisey's Story, Dolores Claiborne, Gerald's Game etc. I'm pretty sure these were all 5 star reads for me! So I had high expectations for Rose Madder, but unfortunately we just didn't click *sad face*<br /><br />My main complaint is the character of Norman, Rose's husband. He's a piece of shit - that much is apparent from the very first page - but as the story progressed, his character became more of a caricature for me? It all became very over the top and exaggerated. I didn't need all the biting... It was enough for me to know that he was an abusive husband - I already hated him, but King kept building on it in a way that I didn't care for. And if Norman had mentioned his ATM card one more time... I think I might have EXPLODED. After a while repetition can become very irritating.<br /><br />Norman is also not a typical King villain. Often King's characters are quite complex, they exist in shades of grey, people aren't always just good or evil. But Norman has ZERO redeeming features - he's racist, homophobic, batters his wife, squeezes people's intestines until they burst. It was very uncomfortable to read the story from his perspective. <br /><br />I also HATED that all the sections from Norman's point of view were in italics. PAGES OF IT. Reading italics for so long hurts my eyes and I can't concentrate - it actually stopped me from picking up the book at times or I would get very easily distracted by my phone instead. I would like to think we're all smart enough to quickly pick up on who's part of the story we're reading without needing it to be made obvious for us.<br /><br />Now, what I DID like - Rosie! She's the kickass female I want to root for! Her relationship with Bill was truly beautiful and I felt very protective over those guys. They are definitely one of King's strongest couples, they were very well-written and believable. I also loved the usual King easter eggs with references to Misery and the Dark Tower series in particular. So bonus points for that!<br /><br />The plot does go down quite a weird route, and initially I felt a bit unimpressed and bored reading it, but over time began to appreciate it more for what it was? I just feel like the book would have worked better if it had stayed focus on the domestic violence aspect, and Rose escaping from her abusive marriage, the events involving the painting just felt a tad stupid at times and over-written. It's just not a new favourite for me unfortunately, but I can champion Rose as another awesome leading lady. And I did enjoy quite large sections of it!<br /><br />3.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17476254469948861802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_8467814" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8467814" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/441351-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1192062504p2/441351.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8467814?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/441351-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">thriller fans, horror fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/441351-mary?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/441351-mary?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8467814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12736174476696528021">Probably my favorite King book, <i>Rose Madder</i> contains some of his creepiest imagery and the best characterization of a woman that he's managed thus far. It's hard not to get involved in Rosie's problems as she runs from a horrifically abusive marriage. The supernatural horror aspect of the story doesn't even enter into it until fairly late in the book; King gives you the chance to watch Rosie grow and change, and to set the stage for what will happen next.<br/><br/>Definitely the one I would loan to someon</span>
  <span id="freeText12736174476696528021" style="display:none">Probably my favorite King book, <i>Rose Madder</i> contains some of his creepiest imagery and the best characterization of a woman that he's managed thus far. It's hard not to get involved in Rosie's problems as she runs from a horrifically abusive marriage. The supernatural horror aspect of the story doesn't even enter into it until fairly late in the book; King gives you the chance to watch Rosie grow and change, and to set the stage for what will happen next.<br /><br />Definitely the one I would loan to someone who wasn't sure about King's more horrifying books, but wanted to try out his writing anyway.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12736174476696528021" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2905403880" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4029839975696965779">There’s a reason why King is one of the best writers around. <br/>Even when the stories aren’t the strongest, it’s the strength of the characters that help propel the constant reader through.<br/><br/>Straight from the prologue where the novel introduces young Rosie Daniels most shockingly brutal attack from her husband Norman, the reader instantly roots for her to leave this domestic hell.<br/>It not until years later after constant abuse that Rosie spots a drop of blood on her bedding that the realisation of this</span>
  <span id="freeText4029839975696965779" style="display:none">There’s a reason why King is one of the best writers around. <br />Even when the stories aren’t the strongest, it’s the strength of the characters that help propel the constant reader through.<br /><br />Straight from the prologue where the novel introduces young Rosie Daniels most shockingly brutal attack from her husband Norman, the reader instantly roots for her to leave this domestic hell.<br />It not until years later after constant abuse that Rosie spots a drop of blood on her bedding that the realisation of this horrific situation will kill her.<br /><br />Fleeing to a next town and fitting the sanctuary of a women’s institute is just the start of a new like for her.<br /><br />King does introduce a supernatural element to the middle section of the book, whilst feeling slightly off kilter to the rest of the novel. <br />Though this section does feel relevant as it harks back to the beginning of the story, whist also a nice sense of foreshadowing. Plus The Dark Tower references were also great.<br /><br />Norman is one of the best ‘worst’ villains that King has created and the switching between him and Rosie through the main part of the book were incredibly well written.<br /><br />By no means Kings best work but there was so much to enjoy, gripping characters mixed with a tough subject matter helps makes this a memorable entry in the King cannon.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1196821781?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" href="/user/show/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder">Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 0451188764" href="/book/show/2992342-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=a-own-book-form">a-own-book-form</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=got-in-2015">got-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=ghosts">ghosts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=mindbender">mindbender</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=monsters">monsters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=unusual-creatures">unusual-creatures</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=magic-powers">magic-powers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1196821781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14747649215695373894"><b>“The concept of dreaming is known to the waking mind but to the dreamer there is no waking, no real world, no sanity; there is only the screaming bedlam of sleep.”</b><br/><br/>Stephen King can be downright weird with surprises he leaves for the reader. For the bulk of the book, it's an interesting story of an abused woman escaping her sadistic and tormenting husband. The main character is a sympathetic lead who doesn't indulge in melodrama or denial, but comes across realistically written when she escapes in</span>
  <span id="freeText14747649215695373894" style="display:none"><b>“The concept of dreaming is known to the waking mind but to the dreamer there is no waking, no real world, no sanity; there is only the screaming bedlam of sleep.”</b><br /><br />Stephen King can be downright weird with surprises he leaves for the reader. For the bulk of the book, it's an interesting story of an abused woman escaping her sadistic and tormenting husband. The main character is a sympathetic lead who doesn't indulge in melodrama or denial, but comes across realistically written when she escapes into a town and group that accepts her for who she is and not what she's escaping. The side characters were as well-written, including the lead of the women's group who, while being noble, isn't black and white noble. 						<br /><br />Rose Madder was a tough book to put down and I didn't get the griping I'd heard about it....until, well until it just gets weird. The painting and other world stuff threw me. I had a discussion with a friend about this and we both felt the story didn't need any of it. He could have just written a thriller with a man chasing his escaped wife and it would have been great; when he introduces the fantasy stuff that was hard to comprehend, the story loses focus and even warrants a little skimming. 						<br /><br />Unlike some of King's other tomes, the length and pacing are suitable to the story content. The ending has a weird and bitter twist which fit well, even again that I didn't full get it. The author doesn't shy away from actual vicious abuse, giving his main villain a biting tendency. I would have liked to see more of the details of the shelter, but I liked the women who worked together and had friendships. It was convincing and thoughtful.						</span>
  <a data-text-id="14747649215695373894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2065844641?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670858699" href="/book/show/335370.Rose_Madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=suspense-thriller">suspense-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2065844641" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer49036727970247923">I just met crazy rivaling that of Annie Wilkes. Norman Daniels. He’s a special kind of nut.<br/><br/>Turns out this book was better than I had anticipated. Maybe that’s due to low expectations, seeing that many put this one towards the bottom of their personal King ratings. Introducing a preternatural painting within a story about spousal abuse is “out there”, maybe more so than his other books. But isn’t that what King does so well? The painting did not necessarily overwhelm the narrative. It added a dim</span>
  <span id="freeText49036727970247923" style="display:none">I just met crazy rivaling that of Annie Wilkes. Norman Daniels. He’s a special kind of nut.<br /><br />Turns out this book was better than I had anticipated. Maybe that’s due to low expectations, seeing that many put this one towards the bottom of their personal King ratings. Introducing a preternatural painting within a story about spousal abuse is “out there”, maybe more so than his other books. But isn’t that what King does so well? The painting did not necessarily overwhelm the narrative. It added a dimension. Some have said this book would have been better as a straight-out thriller, with the supernatural element removed. Guess what? That’s been done - a million times over.<br /><br />There’s a bull parallel with Norman that comes to fruition towards the end. When you see it, it’s an obvious analogy, but smart (you’ll need to have read the book to understand), and it caught me off guard. By doing this King brings the story full circle, using that wacky and wonderful imagination no less. Granted, this book is not for everyone. Even some King fans will cringe at the violence and sexual innuendos (many of which are not innuendos at all). And I don’t know if it was just me, but I was rolling on the floor when Norman got hurt. Norman falls down the stairs. I laughed. Couldn’t help it. I attribute it to King’s use of slapstick amid terror, along with a display of alternating viewpoints (first experienced from Rosie’s point of view, then Norman’s). Plus, Norman’s an ass, deserving of any pain that happened to come his way.<br /><br />Recommend for King fans because none should be missed. A “probably not” for 1st time King readers.<br /><br />PS. There’s a tiny glimpse into the world of The Dark Tower. It doesn’t say much, just kind of fun to run into.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="49036727970247923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1235625078" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1235625078" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1235625078?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670858699" href="/book/show/335370.Rose_Madder">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Stephen King fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Edward by: </span>
      Mom
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1235625078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14383731518547108077">Now I remember why I didn't like this the first time I read it. It wears out its welcome a good 60 pages before the end. We get our denouement, and then we're made to wade through a goodly chunk of book before we can call it done. <br/><br/>Still, <i>Rose Madder</i> is okay. I think what keeps this book pretty middle of the road for me is Norman Daniels, our cliched villain. King has three types of male antagonists: women beaters, child molesters, and racists. Norman Daniels suffers from the former and the latte</span>
  <span id="freeText14383731518547108077" style="display:none">Now I remember why I didn't like this the first time I read it. It wears out its welcome a good 60 pages before the end. We get our denouement, and then we're made to wade through a goodly chunk of book before we can call it done. <br /><br />Still, <i>Rose Madder</i> is okay. I think what keeps this book pretty middle of the road for me is Norman Daniels, our cliched villain. King has three types of male antagonists: women beaters, child molesters, and racists. Norman Daniels suffers from the former and the latter while having also been molested as a child. I'm not a huge fan of the whole molested-people-turn-into-monsters storyline. I know it happens, that the cycle can continue (not all the time, but it does happen), I just don't like reading about it. I would much rather read about someone <i>overcoming</i> their past instead of <i>becoming</i> it. I like to see damaged children beat the odds. Call me an optimist in that regard. <br /><br />Things this book does well are as follows: awesome protagonist (Rosie is one of King's best female characters, says this guy [cue eye rolls]); the gore toward the end of the book is classic King and disturbing as hell; the Dark Tower tie-ins; the thematic elements; the bull mask. Yeah, I dug all that. And if you dig King, I think you will too.<br /><br />Expect spoilers for several King books if you click on View Spoiler. You've been warned.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Obvious Tie-Ins:<br />Ka<br />Paul Sheldon (his <i>Misery</i> novels are mentioned half a dozen times throughout the book)<br />Susan Day (<i>Insomnia</i>)<br />Rose Madder turns into a spider creature, much like Pennywise and Mordred.<br /><br />Theoretical Tie-Ins:<br />Any of you that read my <i>Decade with King</i> posts will know that I believe all of King's books tie into one of three things: <i>It</i>, <i>The Tommyknockers</i>, or the Dark Tower series. Well, all the glowing green shit in this book makes me think the Temple of the Bull might be somehow connected to the Great Old Ones from the Prim. Perhaps the temple is where the Grays were worshipped once upon a when? It does beg the question...  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />In summation: There are much better Stephen King books, there are much worse Stephen King books. If you read all his books in order from <i>Carrie</i> to (as of writing this review) <i>Revival</i>, you should hit this one about the right time. In other words, I would place it smack dab in the middle, exactly where it lands in his career's timeline. <br /><br /><b>Final Judgment: Not everything in this temple is bullshit.</b><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14383731518547108077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15443504999080486219">This is definately my least favorite of the King books I've read so far. I simultaniously loved and hated this book. There were no parts that I just liked...seriously I was either loving it or hating it. Clearly since I gave it 3 stars I loved more parts then I hated.<br/><br/>First the good stuff. As usual Kings take on a crazy person is always amazing and terrifying. My first King book was Misery and I just love his crazy villians. <br/><br/>I was actually getting a little bored with the book until Rosie found he</span>
  <span id="freeText15443504999080486219" style="display:none">This is definately my least favorite of the King books I've read so far. I simultaniously loved and hated this book. There were no parts that I just liked...seriously I was either loving it or hating it. Clearly since I gave it 3 stars I loved more parts then I hated.<br /><br />First the good stuff. As usual Kings take on a crazy person is always amazing and terrifying. My first King book was Misery and I just love his crazy villians. <br /><br />I was actually getting a little bored with the book until Rosie found her painting and the weird stuff, that makes me love King so much, started to happen. I was entranced during Rosies whole 'adventure' in the painting trying to find the baby. I wish a few things had been explained a little more but he kind of left me to make up my own mind which is always bittersweet anyways.<br /><br />And, of course, I loved the Misery references in this book. It made me feel like I was part of a special group of people who were in the know. *wink wink*<br /><br />Now what I did not like. There was an aspect of this book that was so beyond reality and completely fake and phoney that I don't think even King pulled it off. His name was Bill. Rosie's heart of gold, love at first sight, commitment loving boyfriend. Hello...LAME! <br /><br />"I've known you for two weeks and spent a total of 3 hours with you but I'm in love with you and I want to handle all of your baggage as well as your completely insane husband and take you away on my white horse." Total bull shit.<br /><br />I kept expecting this dewey-eyed chick-lit cliche to turn into a hard core dose of reality, which is usually Kings specialty. Something a little more...<br />"You know what Rosie, you're great and I really like you but this is a little to much for me." Which would be totally understandable and expected from any normal guy who doesn't want his penis bitten off by Stormin' Norman.<br /><br />Holy Christ...Nora Roberts fans don't even buy this guy. And I'm pretty sure I heard Nicholas Sparks call him a pussy.<br /><br />Keep in mind that not all woman are waiting around for a white knight to rescue them on his valient steed. Some of us want realistic relationships with realistic men. This character almost completely ruined the female empowerment in this book...<br /><br />...almost, until Gertie's scene with Normie in the amusement park. The woman's lib fanatic in me was jumping for joy when she pee'd all over his face but mostly when she called him a queer boy (I think that was her term) and he couldn't believe a woman was talking to him in that respect. Yep, Normie, its a woman...standing up for herself and being strong. Suck it!</span>

<div id="review_1528320197" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1528320197" />
    <a title="Thomas Strömquist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32669562-thomas-str-mquist"><img alt="Thomas Strömquist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590007844p2/32669562.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1528320197?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Strömquist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Strömquist" href="/user/show/32669562-thomas-str-mquist">Thomas Strömquist</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/16442401-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32669562-thomas-str-mquist?shelf=book-collection">book-collection</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1528320197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11331043346917058137">The 31st book in my long-term Stephen King reading project and the first one I had never read before. Considering common opinion (including King's own) and my friends views, I enjoyed more than I thought I would. However, it's far from without issues. <br/><br/>For a long time I had a vague idea about men like Rose's husband. I imagined that somewhere along the way something went wrong and what causes the rest of us to behave like human beings was lost in them. The abundance of them made me refine my theo</span>
  <span id="freeText11331043346917058137" style="display:none">The 31st book in my long-term Stephen King reading project and the first one I had never read before. Considering common opinion (including King's own) and my friends views, I enjoyed more than I thought I would. However, it's far from without issues. <br><br>For a long time I had a vague idea about men like Rose's husband. I imagined that somewhere along the way something went wrong and what causes the rest of us to behave like human beings was lost in them. The abundance of them made me refine my theory and now I think whatever is missing in their heads was never there in the first place. When the rest of humanity evolved, a large group remained primitive, Homo Wasteofspaceis, a pitiful branch on the family tree that later took a turn for the worse... Anyway, much can be said about them, but best is probably to just avoid them altogether. Reading about them is no fun, and it's probably not good for you to read a book being seriously angry all the time. Being inside one's head, well, anyplace is probably more pleasurable.<br><br>Besides all the vitriolic, ugly, hateful thoughts that I would be fine without, I did not get this guy's similes and references at all, at one point he refers to a character that he does not perceive as a threat at all to be "as dangerous as Bambi's friend Thumper". I don't get it at all. What's not to fear?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454617063i/17992455.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Ah, I needed that! I'll stick to the story now.<br><br>The first half of the book is really good and I enjoyed it very much. At the mid-point, though, something changed and I had troubles just keeping focus on what was happening (which really wasn't much) over the course of a few chapters (that felt really long). Not had I more than realized I was drifting - <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ until the Dark tower-references started dropping in. The book reminded me a bit about Lisey's Story, but, when the dream sequences/traveling work well in that one, here I was bored silly in fantasy land for some reason. But this was never a huge problem, because quite soon  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> - the story was back on track. King does play quite a bit with our emotions, but hey, who's not a sucker for a bit of feel-good, 'aww, that's nice', 'how cute', once in a while? <br><br>So, a few words on the ending. It's long. A lot of what is happening is first told from Rose's perspective - and then the very same happenings are told from the husbands perspective. Two faults here; it drags out a lot, and, we're back inside that horrible mind again!<br><br>Because of the ending and the middle part this gets the "liked it" rating from me. Much better than its reputation I would say.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3471503647" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3471503647" />
    <a title="Obsidian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35942740-obsidian"><img alt="Obsidian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509845837p2/35942740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3471503647?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Obsidian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Obsidian" href="/user/show/35942740-obsidian">Obsidian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670858699" href="/book/show/335370.Rose_Madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35942740-obsidian?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35942740-obsidian?shelf=halloween-bingo-2020">halloween-bingo-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35942740-obsidian?shelf=books-for-halloween-bingo-2020">books-for-halloween-bingo-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3471503647" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8178381231227847027">I don't even know what to say about this one. It is fantastic. King combines the everyday horror that an abused woman goes through with actual horror (going to a different reality where a monster stalks you in a maze) and it works. I think that King can when he wants to write women very well. That's why it drives me up the wall when he drops the ball. The characters are vivid and I would have liked it if King gave a nod to them in his other books, but he does not. The writing at times get a bit </span>
  <span id="freeText8178381231227847027" style="display:none">I don't even know what to say about this one. It is fantastic. King combines the everyday horror that an abused woman goes through with actual horror (going to a different reality where a monster stalks you in a maze) and it works. I think that King can when he wants to write women very well. That's why it drives me up the wall when he drops the ball. The characters are vivid and I would have liked it if King gave a nod to them in his other books, but he does not. The writing at times get a bit too "Kingy" a la repetitive like when referring to Norman and his "I want to talk to you up close" mantra. The flow works though. You are hanging on full of tension to see what King does next. The ending was really good and kind of reminded me a bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10566.Lisey_s_Story" title="Lisey's Story by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Lisey's Story</a>. You have a woman coming full circle and deciding what she is going to do next. <br /><br />"Rose Madder" is about 30 year old house wife, Rose Daniels. The book starts off with some vivid and violent imagery and imagery. Rose has been beaten again by her husband Norman. The beating has caused her to miscarry. With Norman looming over her and telling her to be quiet about what was done, Rose goes away in her own head. The book then jumps forward by several years and we have Rose finally "waking up" and realizes if she does not leave him soon, he is going to either kill her or just leave her so incapacitated, she would want Norman to. Rose realizes that after 18 years being married to Norman she needs to find herself again. What follows is Rose (now Rosie) flight from Norman and trying to have something just for herself. Tie in some King and you also get some horror elements included. When Rosie finds a painting that speaks to her called "Rose Madder" things become even more interested. Constant Readers know how much King loves his paintings. <br /><br />The character of Rose McClendon Daniels, later known as Rosie McClendon and sometimes as Rosie Real will grab at you and have you scared for her life. I think King did an excellent job of showing how abusive men can cut a person off from their friends and family and it's just an ugly twosome of each other. I loved the grit we get to see coming from Rosie as the book goes on. The final showdown was epic and I loved it.<br /><br />The other characters in this one, some do well, some do not. I mean it's King, you know not everyone is getting out alive. The horror of what is done to some of them may stay with you. Don't read if you have a weak stomach.<br /><br />The villain in this one, Norman, reminds me a bit of some of King's other antagonist. A man wanting to hurt a woman just because he can (see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10566.Lisey_s_Story" title="Lisey's Story by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Lisey's Story</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10589.Bag_of_Bones" title="Bag of Bones by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Bag of Bones</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7278752.Dolores_Claiborne" title="Dolores Claiborne by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Dolores Claiborne</a>). We do get some insights into what supposedly made Norman this way, but honestly I was not here for it. Some of the things he did to Rosie and others had me at the no sympathy at all mindset. <br /><br />The writing at times is lyrical, though goodness love King, he gets repetitive at times. Also reading some of the italics (I have a hardcover) made it hard at times. I get that was the way to show Norman's thoughts/feelings on past and present going on's, but it gave me a slight headache to switch back and forth between that and the regular font. <br /><br />The flow works a lot in this one. The tension of Rosie's predicament just sticks with you while you are reading. I was sad to leave this one alone last night (hey even I have to sleep) but picked it up right again when I was having my breakfast.<br /><br />The world building in this one was a great Easter Egg for Constant Readers. We have some tie-ins to "The Dark Tower" (the woman or goddess) that Rosie comes across mentions Lud and ka so we know she's part of Roland's world, or at least a world where it is mentioned. We also have mentions of "Misery" and I think possibly "Insomina." But there are also some of King's favorite narrative plot points, a painting that is not all that it seems, a great big beast in a different land/reality, etc. <br /><br />The ending was really good and I thought that it was a great way to wrap things up. <br /><br />I read this one for the "King of Fear" square. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8178381231227847027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_674309517" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/674309517" />
    <a title="Nick Iuppa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22429985-nick-iuppa"><img alt="Nick Iuppa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442896406p2/22429985.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/674309517?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Iuppa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Iuppa" href="/user/show/22429985-nick-iuppa">Nick Iuppa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer674309517&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer674309517" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer825285368459586843">I really believe that Rose Madder contains one of the most relentless, heart stopping pursuits in any novel anywhere, and - in Norman Daniels - the strongest villain in any of King’s work. King doesn’t just describe Norman Daniels’s actions in Rose Madder; he gets inside the monster’s head and in the process shows us the kind of thoughts that motivate a violent murderer. <br/><br/>Rose McClendon married Norman right after high school, suffered a vicious bite from him on their wedding night, and from then </span>
  <span id="freeText825285368459586843" style="display:none">I really believe that Rose Madder contains one of the most relentless, heart stopping pursuits in any novel anywhere, and - in Norman Daniels - the strongest villain in any of King’s work. King doesn’t just describe Norman Daniels’s actions in Rose Madder; he gets inside the monster’s head and in the process shows us the kind of thoughts that motivate a violent murderer. <br /><br />Rose McClendon married Norman right after high school, suffered a vicious bite from him on their wedding night, and from then on her life became one long nightmare of abusive violence. If things weren’t done Norman’s way, done just right, there was hell to pay... and it was physical hell. Rose was beaten for things like not washing the floor thoroughly enough, reading romance novels, and getting pregnant. It was getting pregnant that landed Rose in the hospital, the baby beaten to death within her. She put up with all this for fourteen years, and then one day, when she noticed a drop of blood from last night’s beating on her bed sheet and knew she’d either have to change the sheets yet again or face more abuse, Rosie just walked out of the house forever. She took Norman’s ATM card and split. <br /><br />What follows is really a story of liberation. One that cuts back and forth between Norman’s raging plans to make his wife pay for the effrontery of leaving him and Rose’s escape into a new life. That life turns out to be far more wonderful than she could ever have imagined. It isn’t just the absence of pain; it’s a world of friends, a lover, and a magical painting she discovers in a pawnshop. <br /><br />The painting becomes a key focus in the story. It shows Rose the image of a strong woman standing on a hillside (a woman who inspires Rose to be strong whenever she’s overcome with doubt or fear), and Rose eventually learns that she can step into the panting, and have amazing though sometimes terrifying new experiences there. Meanwhile, as Rose’s new life improves, Norman draws ever closer, just as she knew he would.<br /><br />How is King is able to gain such a keen grasp of the mind of a sicko like Norman? I’m sure there were mountains of research and discussions with experts. Norman is a cop, a great cop, and he’s internalized police procedures to the highest degree. I can see King in long conversations with guys who know the ways and whiles of the police force and its most aberrant practitioners. Still, I think there’s more to it than that. In a word, it’s genius... maybe King’s greatest genius. He’s such a keen observer of people that he’s able to create believable characters even when they’re monsters. He can enter Norman’s mind, piece together his intentions, reconstruct his thoughts, and give us a clear-cut rationalization for all those brutal actions. <br /><br />When Norman shows up, terrorizes, and murders the very people who saved Rose when she arrived in her new city (has to be Chicago), the painting becomes even more alive. Crickets hop out of the scene and into her bed; the painting’s moonlight fills the room even on the darkest nights. Then the image takes over one whole wall of Rosie’s apartment, and she’s lured inside. Rose does a favor for that woman on the hill... a favor that the woman says she will REPAY. <br /><br />It’s at this moment that the story starts to get even stranger than most of us would ever have expected. Up until now it’s been a terrifying and very real tale of pursuit; now it becomes surreal and horrific. The woman (named Rose Madder for the color of her gown and the words scrawled in charcoal on the back of the painting) is not the sweet motherly type we might have imagined. She’s not just there to help Rose; she’s a monster in her own right, stark raving mad and in the process of being consumed by some flesh-crusting illness. But she also serves a liberating purpose because, when Norman chases Rose into the painting, Rose Madder is more than happy to confront him, reveal her truest self, and basically eat him alive. <br /><br />Teetering on the brink of continued violence from then on, Rose Madder exercises scary self-control as she warns Rosie that she has to do certain things to be able to lead a happy life. Oh, and Rose had better get the hell out of the paining while she can. <br /><br />Rose does escape. She marries the wonderful guy she met earlier, has a sweet daughter, but she’s suddenly possessed by a new and terrible temper. Rosie pictures herself carrying out some of the same kinds of cruel actions that Norman performed. (Guess she couldn’t have been that much of a victim without learning about the allure of violence.) And now Rose begins to fear that her anger with turn her into the very being she encountered in the painting. Rose Madder might have been a preview of what Rosie is to become. King sometimes uses symbols and at other times avoids them. In this case it sure looks like the crusts growing on Rose Madder might be emblematic of the anger that has transferred from Norman to her, taking over her life, turning her into the same kind of monster who had so persecuted Rose. <br /><br />Good news. By carefully following the ritualistic steps that Rose Madder outlined for her, Rose overcomes her anger, and then, one day, when performing the very last task out on a hilly slope that does resemble the one in the painting, Rose realizes that she’s overcome her demons. She’s free. She doesn’t have any of the crusted growths that she saw on the crazy woman. She’s not destined to become Rose Madder. HAPPY ENDING. <br /><br />I get the feeling that King is channeling more folks than Norman Daniels in this one. Shakespeare seems to have worked his way in there too... and probably Euripides. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="825285368459586843" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1883173985" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1883173985" />
    <a title="Cody | CodysBookshelf" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf"><img alt="Cody | CodysBookshelf" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592500568p2/56352820.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1883173985?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody | CodysBookshelf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody | CodysBookshelf" href="/user/show/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf">Cody | CodysBookshelf</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781501143687" href="/book/show/27877126-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf?shelf=stephen-king">stephen-king</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1883173985" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3502062151956131759">Stephen King once famously proclaimed himself the Big Mac and fries of literature — meaning his works are popular and enjoyable, albeit perhaps lacking in nourishment. I heartily disagree with that assessment, for the most part. Novels such as <i>IT</i>, <i>Dolores Claiborne</i>, and <i>The Dead Zone</i> are intricate, multi-layered masterstrokes; methinks King is too modest in regards to his own creations. <br/><br/>However. . . the Maine author's observation does hold true in a few select cases. <i>Christine</i> is a barrel of fun</span>
  <span id="freeText3502062151956131759" style="display:none">Stephen King once famously proclaimed himself the Big Mac and fries of literature — meaning his works are popular and enjoyable, albeit perhaps lacking in nourishment. I heartily disagree with that assessment, for the most part. Novels such as <i>IT</i>, <i>Dolores Claiborne</i>, and <i>The Dead Zone</i> are intricate, multi-layered masterstrokes; methinks King is too modest in regards to his own creations. <br /><br />However. . . the Maine author's observation does hold true in a few select cases. <i>Christine</i> is a barrel of fun, but it certainly offers no depth. That's cool. King's 1983 novel about a haunted car is campy horror at its campiest. I think I would put <i>Rose Madder</i> in the Big Mac and fries category, too: while fun and involving, one comes away feeling full but perhaps not particularly satisfied. <br /><br />This is a brutal, hard-edged tale of spousal abuse, escape, and recovery. The main character is Rose, a woman dealt physical and mental trauma from her husband for fourteen years. <i>Rose Madder</i> is her journey to self-discovery and freedom. Like previous novels <i>Gerald's Game</i> and <i>Dolores Claiborne</i>, King takes an unflinching and daring look at femininity and what it takes to be a woman in the modern age. And, for the most part, he succeeds. <br /><br />Perhaps my biggest problem with this story is not the infamous magical painting Rose escapes into (a plot point that didn't work for me the first time around, but I had a bit more fun with it on this reread), but Norman — the abusive husband. This dude is so over-the-top it's unreal. King is a master at creating despicable, terrifying humans; it's nothing short of fascinating that he failed so completely with Norman. He's a walking cliche, and King never takes the time to give the reader any reason to sympathize with him. He's just CRAAAAAAAAAYYYYYY from literally page one, and he only gets worse. Because of that, much of this novel's potential menace is lost. Shame. <br /><br />That said, the mythological elements of this novel . . . are interesting. They don't always work, and sometimes they seem awkwardly juxtaposed with the woman-on-the-run thriller feel, but it's whatever. King would explore escaping into an alternate, mythic world to better effect in <i>Lisey's Story</i>. <br /><br /><i>Rose Madder</i> is Stephen King at his most average. While containing interesting ideas, some captivating prose (especially that prologue — sheeeeesh!), and a serviceable main character in Rose, this novel just feels tired, inessential. At times I got the sense King was getting bored with the story, and was ready to finish the damn thing. Recommended, but perhaps only for King completists. <br /><br /><b>King Connections:</b><br /><br />There are a few tangential connections to Dark Tower, such as references to <i>ka</i> and the City of Lud. <br /><br />Paul Sheldon of <i>Misery</i> fame gets a few generous shoutouts. <br /><br /><b>Favorite Quote:</b> <br /><br /><i>"In that instant she knew what it must feel like to cross a river into a foreign country, and then set fire to the bridge behind you, and stand on the riverbank, watching and breathing deeply as your only chance of retreat went up in smoke.”</i> <br /><br /><b>Up Next</b>:<br /><br />I thought <i>Desperation</i> was next, but I forgot <i>The Green Mile</i> exists. Ha!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3502062151956131759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_391461314" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/391461314" />
    <a title="Jane Stewart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3789528-jane-stewart"><img alt="Jane Stewart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346001994p2/3789528.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/391461314?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane Stewart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane Stewart" href="/user/show/3789528-jane-stewart">Jane Stewart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780143143949" href="/book/show/4029911-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789528-jane-stewart?shelf=suspense-thriller-with-paranormal">suspense-thriller-with-paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789528-jane-stewart?shelf=abused-women">abused-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3789528-jane-stewart?shelf=5-star-other">5-star-other</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer391461314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11287644273975970577">Great suspense. Wife runs from her abusive husband who is now tracking her.<br/><br/>STORY BRIEF:<br/>The first 10% is Rose living with Norman. They’ve been married fourteen years. He bites, stabs, and punches her. (Most of this harm is told rather than shown so it’s a little less painful for the reader.) The next 80% is Rose leaving Norman, surviving with help from an organization, meeting someone, and Norman’s search for her. The organization helps abused women on the run. The women in the organization have </span>
  <span id="freeText11287644273975970577" style="display:none">Great suspense. Wife runs from her abusive husband who is now tracking her.<br /><br />STORY BRIEF:<br />The first 10% is Rose living with Norman. They’ve been married fourteen years. He bites, stabs, and punches her. (Most of this harm is told rather than shown so it’s a little less painful for the reader.) The next 80% is Rose leaving Norman, surviving with help from an organization, meeting someone, and Norman’s search for her. The organization helps abused women on the run. The women in the organization have some good parts in the story.<br /><br />REVIEWER’S OPINION:<br />Most of the book is suspense. I was on the edge of my seat. I didn’t want to stop. I was afraid of this monstrous abusive cop husband Norman. He’s tracking her. He’s ultra smart and cunning. He’s cruel and killing others to get to her. I was fascinated with Norman’s thoughts, his plans, his words, his actions. Parts of the story were weird and different which I loved, like the oddness of Norman using a bull’s head mask. I loved the author’s creativity being inside Norman’s head. This was better than a demon. This was a human monster.<br /><br />I loved how Rose became strong and her actions. I was intrigued with her anger and rage. She started out as a wimp, but she didn’t stay that way. There is a paranormal element, but it’s secondary or a small part of the story. The paranormal idea was ok, but I probably would have liked the story just as well without it. Many suspense stories disappoint me when characters do stupid things to put themselves in danger. None of that here. Almost everyone did smart things - good guys and the bad guy.<br /><br />I was pleased with the story going several years into the future after the main crisis was resolved, like a long epilogue. It was satisfying, and it was a feel good happy ending.<br /><br />One area bothered me. There is a long dream-like scene in the middle of the book. It was a little boring due to a lot of description and it took her a long time to walk somewhere. I wanted the dream to hurry up so I could get back to the story.<br /><br />There are two sex scenes involving men touching men, one of them torture. Another scene has a woman rear-door raped.<br /><br />AUDIOBOOK ISSUES:<br />Great music introducing the bad guy scenes. It was deep and monstrous with words similar to “hate hate...hate...”<br /><br />Sadly I did not like Stephen King narrating the bad guy parts. I like SK as a person, and I kept thinking of SK as a nice guy. His voice does not fit the bad guy. It would be so much better with a deeper voiced male actor doing those scenes, something compatible with the bad guy music. I hope they produce another version with a different male narrator. I was very happy with SK narrating his nonfiction book “On Writing” but not this book.<br /><br />Blair Brown was excellent and easily 5 stars narrating the Rose parts.<br /><br />DATA:<br />Narrative mode: 3rd person Rose and Norman. Unabridged audiobook reading time: 17 hrs and 28 mins. Swearing language: strong including religious swear words, sexual, ethnic, and racial slurs. Sexual language: strong. Number of sex scenes: four. Setting: mostly 1994 a town in eastern U.S. and a large city in the midwest. Book copyright: 1995. Genre: suspense thriller with a little paranormal, abused women. Ending: happy for the main good guys, bad for the bad guy.<br /><br />OTHER BOOKS:<br />For a list of my reviews of other Stephen King books, see my 5 star review of Carrie.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/327925511">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /></span>

<div id="review_2024672194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2024672194" />
    <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads"><img alt="Abbie | ab_reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545340790p2/53055819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2024672194?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abbie | ab_reads" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads">Abbie | ab_reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2024672194" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer228100102387203596">A blend of horrific reality and mythical fantasy, Rose Madder came out of the left field and surprised me in the best way! I've seen a lot of less-than-positive reviews of this particular King, but I loved it. I loved the Greek mythology references, I loved the main character Rosie McClendon, I loved the raw, brutal story, everything!<br/><br/>The prologue really sets the tone for the rest of the novel, it's one of the hardest things I've ever had to read. It's not for the fainthearted and there's a lot o</span>
  <span id="freeText228100102387203596" style="display:none">A blend of horrific reality and mythical fantasy, Rose Madder came out of the left field and surprised me in the best way! I've seen a lot of less-than-positive reviews of this particular King, but I loved it. I loved the Greek mythology references, I loved the main character Rosie McClendon, I loved the raw, brutal story, everything!<br /><br />The prologue really sets the tone for the rest of the novel, it's one of the hardest things I've ever had to read. It's not for the fainthearted and there's a lot of scenes with graphic details of domestic and sexual abuse, so beware. As usual, I cannot fault King's writing; the style is sometimes repetitive especially near the beginning, but I think he perfectly captures the mindset of a terrified, paranoid victim of abuse and, again, shows that he's so much more than just a horror writer. Despite abuse being a prominent plot point, there are also some beautifully poignant passages about love that rival those in Lisey's Story.<br /><br />Of course, there is a fair share of horror in Rose Madder! Norman Daniels is possibly King's worst creation. A despicable, violent, just all round fucked up man, the parts of the narrative told from his perspective are EXTREMELY unsettling and disturbing. There's also some great horror scenes through the painting, which I can see how they could be confusing, but I didn't have a problem with them!<br /><br />As well as Rosie, my favourite part about this novel were all of the King easter eggs!! Constant readers will have an absolute field day here, with ties to Dark Tower, The Talisman, Misery, and maybe even Lisey's Story.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="228100102387203596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200549066?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timbo  Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timbo  Jones" href="/user/show/4427306-timbo-jones">Timbo  Jones</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200549066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3884247195815036842">This is without a doubt the worst Stephen King book I've read. Why he felt the need to add the fantasy element in I don't know, but I found it absolutely ridiculous.<br/><br/>Norman Daniels (the psychopath husband) was almost a caricature more than a character. A purely evil, hateful man with no redeeming qualities whatsoever, yet we're expected to believe this psycopath functions perfectly well in the police force and is a highly decorated detective? Trying to imagine this man being sympathetic and kind </span>
  <span id="freeText3884247195815036842" style="display:none">This is without a doubt the worst Stephen King book I've read. Why he felt the need to add the fantasy element in I don't know, but I found it absolutely ridiculous.<br /><br />Norman Daniels (the psychopath husband) was almost a caricature more than a character. A purely evil, hateful man with no redeeming qualities whatsoever, yet we're expected to believe this psycopath functions perfectly well in the police force and is a highly decorated detective? Trying to imagine this man being sympathetic and kind to victims of serious crime, being a credible witness in court etc is impossible for me to do.<br /><br />It's a shame, because I liked the idea of a much-abused wife finally 'snapping', taking hubby's bank card, then running off to try to start a new life elsewhere and the cop husband trying to track her down. Sure, it's not original, but King could've made it work. Instead, he made a half-arsed (assed, if you're American!)fantasy novel.<br /><br />I'm one of those people who can't give up on a book once I've read past the first few chapters, so I stuck with it til the end. Poor writing, poor characterisation, with ridiculous fantasy elements included. Recommended to absolutely no one; a terrible novel.</span>
