<div id="review_3622806089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3622806089" />
    <a title="Celeste" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste"><img alt="Celeste" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481055226p2/7317186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3622806089?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celeste" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celeste" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste">Celeste</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781501143687" href="/book/show/27877126-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=girl-power">girl-power</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=horror">horror</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3622806089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6241616519765272623">Actual rating: 4.5 stars, rounded up.<br/><br/>This is one of those King books I honestly didn’t expect much from. It’s not one commonly listed as a favorite, or even mentioned that frequently from among his works. I can’t say I would’ve thought to pick it up had I not been so invested in the extended reading list for the <i>Dark Tower</i>. But it was next on that list, so I found myself a copy. Now I’m incredibly glad I picked it up. <b>Though not perfect, <i>Rose Madder</i> is now one of my favorite King novels outside </b></span>
  <span id="freeText6241616519765272623" style="display:none">Actual rating: 4.5 stars, rounded up.<br /><br />This is one of those King books I honestly didn’t expect much from. It’s not one commonly listed as a favorite, or even mentioned that frequently from among his works. I can’t say I would’ve thought to pick it up had I not been so invested in the extended reading list for the <i>Dark Tower</i>. But it was next on that list, so I found myself a copy. Now I’m incredibly glad I picked it up. <b>Though not perfect, <i>Rose Madder</i> is now one of my favorite King novels outside of <i>The Stand</i> and the main <i>Dark Tower </i>series. Talk about a protagonist you can root for.</b><br /><blockquote>“It ain't the blows we're dealt that matter, but the ones we survive.”</blockquote> <br /><u>Rose Daniels has had enough</u>. After 14 years of extreme spousal abuse at the hands of her policeman husband, one drop of blood on a bed sheet is her catalyst for leaving this horrible half-life she’s been living and starting over somewhere new. Now Rosie is finally embarking on a life of her own, for the first time in her adult life. And it’s absolutely wonderful. But Norman Daniels refuses to let his wife go. He’s going to track her down. And when he finds her, he’s going to talk to her. Talk to her right up close. And he plans on it being the last conversation Rose ever has. However, there are odd powers at work here, and Norman is going to find this plan much more difficult to carry out than he ever imagined.<br /><blockquote>“The concept of dreaming is known to the waking mind but to the dreamer there is no waking, no real world, no sanity; there is only the screaming bedlam of sleep.”</blockquote><br /><b>Norman might just be the most despicable fictional character in the history of fiction. </b> He’s one of the most disturbed, chauvinistic, narcissistic, deranged excuse for a human being in whose head I’ve ever had the displeasure of being. I found him appalling. <b>But Rose, on the other hand, is possibly the best female character King has ever created.</b> She feels incredibly real, and both her hopes and her terrible fears rang very true. The way she grows over the course of the novel made me want to cheer. Her resilience and optimism is inspiring.<br /><blockquote>“It's best to be ruthless with the past.”</blockquote> <br />While I developed some deep feelings regarding both protagonist and antagonist, what really sold me on this book was the horror element. I’ve read a lot of King by this point, including most of his horror classics like <i>The Shining</i> and <i>IT</i> and <i>‘Salem’s Lot</i> and <i>Pet Sematary</i>. <b>There are portions of <i>Rose Madder</i> that I truly believe are just as freaky and disturbing as the aforementioned books.</b> And the horror here felt different and original in ways that resonated with me. I won’t soon be forgetting the source of the novel’s title, or why there’s a bull skull on the cover.<br /><blockquote>“That’s really all art is about, I think, and not just pictures—it’s the same with books and stories and sculpture and even castles in the sand. Some things call to us, that’s all. It’s as if the people who made them were speaking inside our heads.”</blockquote><br />I could never call King or his work underrated. But <u>I do think <i>Rose Madder</i> deserves more attention than it gets</u>. I loved seeing the ways in which it’s going to tie into the Tower, but even outside of those connections I found it a fantastic book. The only reason it’s a 4.5 star read for me instead of a full 5 is because Norman’s head was such a vile place to be, even though, thankfully, his perspective chapters were fewer and shorter than Rosie’s. <b><i>Rose Madder</i> is a great horror-laced thriller that will be in my head for a long time to come.</b><br /><br /><i>You can find <a href="https://wp.me/pa0WBY-2CL" rel="nofollow noopener"> this</a> review and more at <a href="http://novelnotions.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions</a>. </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="6241616519765272623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_96220685" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96220685" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1254714-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513279210p2/1254714.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96220685?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/1254714-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 0451188764" href="/book/show/2992342-rose-madder">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1254714-steve?shelf=horror-and-dark-fiction">horror-and-dark-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1254714-steve?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96220685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16952376690903239515"><i>Rose Madder</i> is part of a group of King books I had yet not read. Well, I'm glad I did. Like his best efforts, it is character driven. And in Rose Daniels/McClendon King supplies one of the best characters in all the books I've read by him. On the dark side of things, her abusive husband is one of his darkest villains. What makes both so interesting is that they are also complex. Rose, whose innocence covers a core of rage; and Norman, who for all of his brutality, is ultimately pathetic. What Ki</span>
  <span id="freeText16952376690903239515" style="display:none"><i>Rose Madder</i> is part of a group of King books I had yet not read. Well, I'm glad I did. Like his best efforts, it is character driven. And in Rose Daniels/McClendon King supplies one of the best characters in all the books I've read by him. On the dark side of things, her abusive husband is one of his darkest villains. What makes both so interesting is that they are also complex. Rose, whose innocence covers a core of rage; and Norman, who for all of his brutality, is ultimately pathetic. What King offers up is not a black and white universe. And of course there is a whole slew of lesser but well drawn characters that propel the story along. <br /><br />As a novel, the plotting is pretty good. However, somewhere around page 400 I thought, from a pacing point of view, King should have wrapped things up. But that would of left out Norman's horrible Bacchae-like fate (which practically has you feeling sorry for him - some things ARE worse than death), and an ending that did seem, though strangely cryptic, right. There is some padding, but not nearly enough to give me <i>Tommyknocker</i> nightmares. <br /><br />There is mention here from others about King's <i>Dark Tower</i> series, and the symbolism King employs in <i>Rose Madder</i>. Since I have not read those books, I can't comment on how effectively that works. I am aware of King's statements regarding how he is trying to tie much of his work together in such a way it relates to the <i>Dark Tower</i> series. To some extent I think this is (in a number of cases) an after the fact effort by King - and a mistake. Whatever. By itself, the symbolism of <i>Rose Madder</i> is somewhat confusing, but I think the real-world rooting of the story helps the reader skate over these areas fairly lightly, with entertainment trumping confusion. However, <i>Madder</i> does veer from a dark fairy tale beginning, into Greek mythology, and closes with what some might consider a Christian allegory. Basically, Wrath is a deadly sin, and Norman's fate is linked to that fact. Rose herself is warned of her own susceptibility to anger - even if it is well justified. Rose's remembrance of the "tree" is simply a need to bury the past. Read tree here as "cross," but in a way that supersedes the earlier mythological meaning (King getting deep here). Otherwise, <i>Madder</i> will become madness, as the spider goddess (clearly Diana) warns Rose. Underscoring this Christian take on <i>Madder</i>, is the goddess being restrained by Dorcas (see the New Testament). Good stuff, with King (successfully) stretching himself, while still operating within genre. Not an easy thing to do. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16952376690903239515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18272713166107177689">This contains both the good and bad (well not really bad) of Stephen King. I really loved the part of the book where he descibes Rose's relationship with her abusive husband, how she breaks away finally from that and then runs away from him across the country. Norman, a police officer hunts Rose down and begins to eliminate those who try to interfere with his pursuit of his wife. Great characters who are described in depth.<br/><br/>The bad part of the book begins with the painting Rose buys for her new a</span>
  <span id="freeText18272713166107177689" style="display:none">This contains both the good and bad (well not really bad) of Stephen King. I really loved the part of the book where he descibes Rose's relationship with her abusive husband, how she breaks away finally from that and then runs away from him across the country. Norman, a police officer hunts Rose down and begins to eliminate those who try to interfere with his pursuit of his wife. Great characters who are described in depth.<br /><br />The bad part of the book begins with the painting Rose buys for her new apartment and the fantasy adventure she has going into the painting. Too weird and the story just isn't tied up they way you would like it at the end.<br /><br />Stephen King is my favorite author but when he travels down the fantasy route he often leaves my interest. You do see the man's talent however.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18272713166107177689" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9563311219187783560">4.5 Stars <br/><br/>A really powerful book! The first chapter of this book was horrible and disturbing and not for the faint hearted. King never fails to create the most despicable characters and this book is no different. The start of this book really is shocking and vivid and I did have to question if I wanted to read on but I am glad I did! More of a thriller than a horror I would say but….   <span id="freeText9563311219187783560" style="display:none">4.5 Stars <br /><br />A really powerful book! The first chapter of this book was horrible and disturbing and not for the faint hearted. King never fails to create the most despicable characters and this book is no different. The start of this book really is shocking and vivid and I did have to question if I wanted to read on but I am glad I did! More of a thriller than a horror I would say but…. Horrific all the same!<br /><br />I will repeat that this isn’t for the faint-hearted really some of these intense reliving descriptions are really hard to read. Norman is the worst character EVER and Stephen King really makes you hate him and it isn’t just the way he treats rose but how he is with everyone and everything. A true horror and what makes him more horrific is that he could be anywhere! <br /><br />I hope this book does help women going through abuse and they know that it isn’t ok and there is always a way OUT! <b> RefugeThe freephone, 24-hour National Domestic Abuse Helpline 0808 2000 247 </b> <br /><br />All you can think when reading this book is how you want Norman to get everything that is coming to him! Plenty of action in this book which I LOVE! <br /><br />The only other thing I could think is how much I hope Rose finds closure, realisation and happiness! <br />"But she stayed where she was a moment longer, like an animal which has been kept in a cage so long it cannot believe in freedom even when it is offered."<br /><br />"She didn't like seeing that look in his face, but she wanted to see it there, she did. He was a man, wasn't he? And sometimes men had to learn what it was to be afraid of a woman." (YESSSSSS GIRL)<br />The structure of the book is also brilliant! It flicks between both characters and they are such a contrast! This book was easy to read – with its short chapters and it helped that I just needed to know what was going to happen! <br /><br />The little nods to The Dark Tower were brilliant to read – I love that Stephen Kings book interact each other! <br /><br />This is my first read of Rose Madder and I don’t think it will be my last another horrifically brilliant King book and I’m far from disappointed! One day I will have read his entire book collection! I have a lot of catching up to do wish me luck! <br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4271022229352288007">A wonderfully weighted book in the first two acts as King really gets into the underbelly of domestic abuse from victim to escapee. A hard read, but also with so much positivism, especially the impact and power of kindness and empathy. However, what ruins the book for me was the third act which shoots into magical realism, I kid you not! As I got older I got to tolerate and even understand King's rationale, but back when I first read this, I was soooo annoyed!<br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1577801382ra/28693993.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>My 2005 review shot this up to 8 out</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/29339656?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kandice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kandice" href="/user/show/1396160-kandice">Kandice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29339656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4149843040908525173">I'm not sure how many times I have read this book, but this time I listened to it read by Blair Brown and Stephen King. I know I gave it five stars before, but I don't feel that is really enough. This book is fantastic! King is so very good at writing from the point of view of the "other." Here, he not only writes from the point of a woman who is a victim of horrific spousal abuse, but he also writes from the point of view of the abuser. I cannot even begin to imagine that Tabby would put up wit</span>
  <span id="freeText4149843040908525173" style="display:none">I'm not sure how many times I have read this book, but this time I listened to it read by Blair Brown and Stephen King. I know I gave it five stars before, but I don't feel that is really enough. This book is fantastic! King is so very good at writing from the point of view of the "other." Here, he not only writes from the point of a woman who is a victim of horrific spousal abuse, but he also writes from the point of view of the abuser. I cannot even begin to imagine that Tabby would put up with any kind of abuse. She'd kick his ass quicker than he'd draw a fist, so both POVs are of the "other." And yet both ring incredibly true. Beautifully true.<br /><br />Rose Daniels has been married to Norman, her cop hubby, for more than a decade and a half and he is an incredibly inventive abuser. Not only does he know exactly where and how to hit without leaving a mark, but he bites. I cannot even imagine being bitten in this way. I can imagine a punch, a kick, a pinch... but a bite? God forbid! The novel opens with a frightening incident that takes place years before the meat of the tale, but it sets the stage so perfectly. We see just what Rose lives through and when she "wakes up" one day, how crucial it is that she escape quickly and leaves no trace. The fact that despite her terror she finds the fortitude to do so is incredible.<br /><br />Because it happens in the first few pages, it's not really spoiling to say Rosie does escape. She has some help from some very good people, but most of all she helps herself. She finds a job that Norman has trained her to do better by beating the crap out of her on the regular. How ironic is that. A great big "screw you" to him! I love that King includes this small detail. Sadly, Norman is never going to be happy with letting her go so he finds her.<br /><br />Like so many of King's books there is some real fantasy what-the-fuckery going on here. These parts are my least favorite scenes of the book. It doesn't detract from the mastery of the writing, but I felt it was worth mentioning. This is one of the very few books of his that actually has a happy ending. If anyone deserves one, it's Rosie Real, Really Rosie.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4149843040908525173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_16575275" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16575275" />
    <a title="Megan Lucas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/950419-megan-lucas"><img alt="Megan Lucas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216959866p2/950419.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16575275?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Lucas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Lucas" href="/user/show/950419-megan-lucas">Megan Lucas</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16575275" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7390561927296392135">While reading this book I kept forgetting that it was a Stephen King novel. It has it's twists and turns (as his usual work does) but this was so much more than good vs. evil. Rose Madder is an emotional account of an abused woman who is trying to break free from a tormenting life, and has what is portrayed as a psychological break through as she goes on a journey to be free. King also touches on the downfalls of the system that fails to protect women from situations like Rose is in. Unfortunate</span>
  <span id="freeText7390561927296392135" style="display:none">While reading this book I kept forgetting that it was a Stephen King novel. It has it's twists and turns (as his usual work does) but this was so much more than good vs. evil. Rose Madder is an emotional account of an abused woman who is trying to break free from a tormenting life, and has what is portrayed as a psychological break through as she goes on a journey to be free. King also touches on the downfalls of the system that fails to protect women from situations like Rose is in. Unfortunately there is some graphical and language issues throughout the book, but I would expect to see them in this type of dialogue. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7390561927296392135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_225099879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225099879" />
    <a title="Madiha Riaz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6461909-madiha-riaz"><img alt="Madiha Riaz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317191029p2/6461909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225099879?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madiha Riaz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madiha Riaz" href="/user/show/6461909-madiha-riaz">Madiha Riaz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Madiha by: </span>
      My sister
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6461909-madiha-riaz?shelf=dear-writer-what-were-u-thinking">dear-writer-what-were-u-thinking</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6461909-madiha-riaz?shelf=such-a-disappointing-read">such-a-disappointing-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225099879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10592509103053849447">A fine example of books that start out brilliantly, lose the grip somewhere in between, and just go downhill from there. I cannot believe I finished it. My only motivation behind finishing it was to find out if the end is really what I was thinking of. And no, it was worse.<br/><br/>An abused wife runs away from home; the descriptive scenes, the in-depth psychological insights about the male and female protagonists were just awesome. They hooked to me the book. And I had heard so much about Stephen King, </span>
  <span id="freeText10592509103053849447" style="display:none">A fine example of books that start out brilliantly, lose the grip somewhere in between, and just go downhill from there. I cannot believe I finished it. My only motivation behind finishing it was to find out if the end is really what I was thinking of. And no, it was worse.<br /><br />An abused wife runs away from home; the descriptive scenes, the in-depth psychological insights about the male and female protagonists were just awesome. They hooked to me the book. And I had heard so much about Stephen King, I was so thrilled to find myself finally reading him. What a disappointment!<br /><br />What started out as an abused wife's escape from a demented and mentally sick husband, soon turned into something boringly-Halloween. I mean, the heroine actually going INTO the painting! The writer did not explain the relation of Rosie with the woman in the picture, and why was it necessary for Rosie to go into the picture in the first place, was unclear to me. What was the role of the fox in the novel? The heroin does not get the final revenge, and then she turns against her boyfriend and directs her rage towards him? The lovable heroin, that writer so beautifully and strongly portrayed in the beginning was lost, and what came out was just crap. <br /><br />and the end!!! My God, could it be more abysmal than that? That was the most disappointing end to any story I have ever read. It was made even more disappointing by the fact that I really really really liked the book initially. Such a waste of time.<br /><br />Anyway, I'm not going to let this one experience put me off of Stephen King forever. I'm going to give him another chance:) I've started reading Carrie - lets see if it helps me improve this writer's ratings.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10592509103053849447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('USrEbXgMmIDPWekkas3wlUORJLQn6ACzN6tDnFBTrZfR9HkLu7uy3AWl7iB0vX42Y/JlR1MLa1747rCnvah/Zw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WNjmv9AaiOm7hGLHfYGOcP0Q/GrxOMAXPtp7cpyHVIHYBlvZE62itXF4ZcNj8QDT3XO9mYXbq/rxn4hJcXyGcQ==')})
  }

Clayton Blaisdell, Jr., was always a small-time delinquent. None too bright either, thanks to the beatings he got as a kid. Then Blaze met George Rackley, a seasoned pro with a hundred cons and one big idea. The kidnapping should go off without a hitch, with George as the brains behind their dangerous scheme.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">About Stephen King</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1557446886379460098">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, M</span>
  <span id="freeText1557446886379460098" style="display:none">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, Maine, for good. Her parents, Guy and Nellie Pillsbury, had become incapacitated with old age, and Ruth King was persuaded by her sisters to take over the physical care of them. Other family members provided a small house in Durham and financial support. After Stephen's grandparents passed away, Mrs. King found work in the kitchens of Pineland, a nearby residential facility for the mentally challenged.<br /><br />Stephen attended the grammar school in Durham and Lisbon Falls High School, graduating in 1966. From his sophomore year at the University of Maine at Orono, he wrote a weekly column for the school newspaper, THE MAINE CAMPUS. He was also active in student politics, serving as a member of the Student Senate. He came to support the anti-war movement on the Orono campus, arriving at his stance from a conservative view that the war in Vietnam was unconstitutional. He graduated in 1970, with a B.A. in English and qualified to teach on the high school level. A draft board examination immediately post-graduation found him 4-F on grounds of high blood pressure, limited vision, flat feet, and punctured eardrums.<br /><br />He met Tabitha Spruce in the stacks of the Fogler Library at the University, where they both worked as students; they married in January of 1971. As Stephen was unable to find placement as a teacher immediately, the Kings lived on his earnings as a laborer at an industrial laundry, and her student loan and savings, with an occasional boost from a short story sale to men's magazines.<br /><br />Stephen made his first professional short story sale ("The Glass Floor") to <i>Startling Mystery Stories</i> in 1967. Throughout the early years of his marriage, he continued to sell stories to men's magazines. Many were gathered into the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10628.Night_Shift" title="Night Shift" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Shift</a> collection or appeared in other anthologies.<br /><br />In the fall of 1971, Stephen began teaching English at Hampden Academy, the public high school in Hampden, Maine. Writing in the evenings and on the weekends, he continued to produce short stories and to work on novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1557446886379460098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
